					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Edible fruit</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other species of strawberry, see <a href="/wiki/Fragaria" title="Fragaria">Fragaria</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Strawberry (disambiguation)">Strawberry (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><b><span class="binomial"><span style="font-weight:normal;"></span><i>Fragaria <span style="font-style:normal;">×</span>&#32;ananassa</i></span></b><br /><div style="font-size: 85%;"><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Nicolas_Duchesne" title="Antoine Nicolas Duchesne">Duchesne</a></div>
</td></tr>


































</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1220493105">html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}</style>
<p>The <b>garden strawberry</b> (or simply <b>strawberry</b>; <i><b>Fragaria × ananassa</b></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Manganaris2014_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manganaris2014-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is a widely grown <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid species</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Fragaria" title="Fragaria">Fragaria</a></i>, collectively known as the strawberries, which are cultivated worldwide for their <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>. The fruit is widely appreciated for its characteristic aroma, bright red color, juicy texture, and sweetness. It is consumed in large quantities, either fresh or in such prepared foods as <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">jam</a>, juice, <a href="/wiki/Pie" title="Pie">pies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milkshake" title="Milkshake">milkshakes</a>, and chocolates. Artificial strawberry flavorings and aromas are also widely used in products such as candy, soap, <a href="/wiki/Lip_gloss" title="Lip gloss">lip gloss</a>, perfume, and many others.
</p><p>The garden strawberry was first bred in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, France, in the 1750s via a cross of <i><a href="/wiki/Virginia_strawberry" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia strawberry">Fragaria virginiana</a></i> from eastern North America and <i><a href="/wiki/Fragaria_chiloensis" title="Fragaria chiloensis">Fragaria chiloensis</a></i>, which was brought from Chile by <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9d%C3%A9e-Fran%C3%A7ois_Fr%C3%A9zier" title="Amédée-François Frézier">Amédée-François Frézier</a> in 1714.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">Cultivars</a> of <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> have replaced, in commercial production, the woodland strawberry (<i><a href="/wiki/Fragaria_vesca" title="Fragaria vesca">Fragaria vesca</a></i>), which was the first strawberry species cultivated in the early 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-welsh1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welsh1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From a botanical point of view, the strawberry is not a <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a> but an <a href="/wiki/Aggregate_fruit" title="Aggregate fruit">aggregate</a> <a href="/wiki/Accessory_fruit" title="Accessory fruit">accessory</a> fruit, meaning that the fleshy part is derived not from the plant's <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovaries</a> but from the <a href="/wiki/Receptacle_(botany)" title="Receptacle (botany)">receptacle</a> that holds the ovaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Esau_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esau-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Each apparent "seed" (<a href="/wiki/Achene" title="Achene">achene</a>) on the outside of the fruit is actually one of the ovaries of the flower, with a seed inside it.<sup id="cite_ref-Esau_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esau-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, world production of strawberries was nine million tons, led by China with 40% of the total.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Breeding_of_strawberries" title="Breeding of strawberries">Breeding of strawberries</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Closeup of a healthy, red strawberry" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG/220px-Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG/330px-Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG/440px-Strawberry_gariguette_DSC03063.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1412" data-file-height="1068" /></a><figcaption><i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> 'Gariguette,' a cultivar grown in southern France</figcaption></figure>
<p>The first garden strawberry was grown in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, France, during the late 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-welsh1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welsh1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Prior to this, <a href="/wiki/Fragaria" title="Fragaria">wild strawberries</a> and cultivated selections from wild strawberry species were the common source of the fruit.
</p><p>The strawberry fruit was mentioned in ancient Roman literature in reference to its medicinal use. The French began taking the strawberry from the forest to their gardens for harvest in the 14th century. <a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">Charles V</a>, France's king from 1364 to 1380, had 1,200 strawberry plants in his royal garden. In the early 15th century western European monks were using the wild strawberry in their illuminated manuscripts. The strawberry is found in Italian, Flemish, and German art, and in English miniatures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The entire strawberry plant was used to treat depressive illnesses.
</p><p>By the 16th century, references of cultivation of the strawberry became more common. People began using it for its supposed medicinal properties and botanists began naming the different species. In England the demand for regular strawberry farming had increased by the mid-16th century.
</p><p>The combination of strawberries and cream was created by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolsey" title="Thomas Wolsey">Thomas Wolsey</a> in the court of King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wimbledon_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wimbledon-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Instructions for growing and harvesting strawberries showed up in writing in 1578. By the end of the 16th century three European species had been cited: <i>F. vesca</i>, <i>F. moschata</i>, and <i>F. viridis</i>. The garden strawberry was transplanted from the forests and then the plants would be propagated <a href="/wiki/Asexual_propagation" class="mw-redirect" title="Asexual propagation">asexually</a> by cutting off the <a href="/wiki/Stolon" title="Stolon">runners</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Strawberry leaves" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg/220px-Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg/330px-Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg/440px-Two_strawberry_leaves_sunny.jpg 2x" data-file-width="811" data-file-height="814" /></a><figcaption>Strawberry leaves</figcaption></figure>
<p>Two subspecies of <i>F. vesca</i> were identified: <i>F. sylvestris alba</i> and <i>F. sylvestris semperflorens</i>. The introduction of <i>F. virginiana</i> from eastern North America to Europe in the 17th century is an important part of history because it is one of the two species that gave rise to the modern strawberry. The new species gradually spread through the continent and did not become completely appreciated until the end of the 18th century. A French excursion journeyed to Chile in 1712, which led to the introduction of a strawberry plant with female flowers that resulted in the common strawberry.
</p><p>The Mapuche and Huilliche Indians of Chile cultivated the female strawberry species until 1551, when the Spanish came to conquer the land. In 1765, a European explorer recorded the cultivation of <i>F. chiloensis</i>, the Chilean strawberry. At first introduction to Europe, the plants grew vigorously, but produced no fruit.  French gardeners in Brest and <a href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> around the mid-18th century first noticed that when <i>F. moschata</i> and <i>F. virginiana</i> were planted in between rows of <i>F. chiloensis</i>, the Chilean strawberry would bear abundant and unusually large fruits. Soon after, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Nicolas_Duchesne" title="Antoine Nicolas Duchesne">Antoine Nicolas Duchesne</a> began to study the <a href="/wiki/Breeding_of_strawberries" title="Breeding of strawberries">breeding of strawberries</a> and made several discoveries crucial to the science of plant breeding, such as the sexual reproduction of the strawberry which he published in 1766. Duchesne discovered that the female <i>F. chiloensis</i> plants could only be pollinated by male <i>F. moschata</i> or <i>F. virginiana</i> plants.<sup id="cite_ref-duchesne_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duchesne-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This is when the Europeans became aware that plants had the ability to produce male-only or female-only flowers.
</p><p>Duchesne determined <i>F. ananassa</i> to be a hybrid of <i>F. chiloensis</i> and <i>F. virginiana</i>. <i>F. ananassa</i>, which produces large fruits, is so named because it resembles the pineapple in smell, taste and berry shape. In England, many varieties of <i>F. ananassa</i> were produced, and they form the basis of modern varieties of strawberries currently cultivated and consumed. Further breeding was also conducted in Europe and America to improve the hardiness, disease resistance, size, and taste.<sup id="cite_ref-duchesne_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duchesne-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> of the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">agronomists</a> used selective breeding to expand <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> diversity of the garden strawberry. Adoption of perpetual flowering hybrids not sensitive to changes in <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">photoperiod</a> gave higher yields and expansion of production in California.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1218072481"></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><b style="margin-left:-0.65em">Other constituents</b></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;"><b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">90.95 g</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/fdc-app.html#/food-details/747448/nutrients">Link to USDA Database entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite_ref-FDADailyValues_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">the National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Raw strawberries are 91% water, 8% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, and contain negligible fat (table). A 100&#160;gram reference amount of strawberries supplies 33 <a href="/wiki/Kilocalorie" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilocalorie">kilocalories</a>, is a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> (71% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>, DV), a good source of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (18% DV), and provides several other vitamins and dietary minerals in small amounts. Strawberries contain a modest amount of essential <a href="/wiki/Unsaturated_fatty_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Unsaturated fatty acid">unsaturated fatty acids</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Achene" title="Achene">achene</a> (seed) oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Giampieri2012_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giampieri2012-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</span></h3>
<p>Garden strawberries contain the dimeric <a href="/wiki/Ellagitannin" title="Ellagitannin">ellagitannin</a> agrimoniin which is an isomer of <a href="/wiki/Sanguiin_H-6" title="Sanguiin H-6">sanguiin H-6</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lipinska2014_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lipinska2014-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Other polyphenols present include <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flavanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavanol">flavanols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flavonol" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavonol">flavonols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenolic_acid" title="Phenolic acid">phenolic acids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydroxybenzoic_acid" title="Hydroxybenzoic acid">hydroxybenzoic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxycinnamic_acid" title="Hydroxycinnamic acid">hydroxycinnamic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Giampieri2012_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giampieri2012-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Strawberries contain <a href="/wiki/Fisetin" title="Fisetin">fisetin</a> and possess higher levels of this flavonoid than other fruits.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khan2013_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan2013-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Although achenes comprise only about 1% of total fresh weight of a strawberry, they contribute 11% of the total polyphenol in the whole fruit; achene phytochemicals include <a href="/wiki/Ellagic_acid" title="Ellagic acid">ellagic acid</a>, ellagic acid <a href="/wiki/Glycoside" title="Glycoside">glycosides</a>, and ellagitannins.<sup id="cite_ref-aaby_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaby-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color">Color</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pelargonidin-3-glucoside" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelargonidin-3-glucoside">Pelargonidin-3-glucoside</a> is the major anthocyanin in strawberries and <a href="/wiki/Cyanidin-3-glucoside" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyanidin-3-glucoside">cyanidin-3-glucoside</a> is found in smaller proportions. Although glucose seems to be the most common substituting sugar in strawberry anthocyanins, rutinose, arabinose, and rhamnose conjugates have been found in some strawberry cultivars.<sup id="cite_ref-Giampieri2012_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giampieri2012-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Purple minor pigments consisting of dimeric anthocyanins (flavanol-anthocyanin adducts&#160;: catechin(4α→8)pelargonidin 3-O-β-glucopyranoside, epicatechin(4α→8)pelargonidin 3-O-β-glucopyranoside, afzelechin(4α→8)pelargonidin 3-O-β-glucopyranoside and epiafzelechin(4α→8)pelargonidin 3-O-β-glucopyranoside) can also be found in strawberries.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flavor_and_fragrance">Flavor and fragrance</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Furaneol.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Furaneol.svg/130px-Furaneol.svg.png" decoding="async" width="130" height="96" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Furaneol.svg/195px-Furaneol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Furaneol.svg/260px-Furaneol.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="411" data-file-height="305" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Furaneol" title="Furaneol">Furaneol</a> is an important component of the fragrance of strawberries.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sweetness, fragrance and complex flavor are favorable attributes.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a> and farming, emphasis is placed on sugars, acids, and <a href="/wiki/Essential_oils" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential oils">volatile</a> compounds, which improve the taste and fragrance of a ripe strawberry.<sup id="cite_ref-UFla_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFla-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Although often sweet tasting, strawberries actually have relatively low sugar levels compared to other fruits. <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">Esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">furans</a> are the chemical compounds having the strongest relationships to strawberry flavor, sweetness and fragrance, with a total of 31 out of some 360 volatile compounds significantly correlated to favorable flavor and fragrance.<sup id="cite_ref-UFla_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFla-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Negri_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Negri-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In breeding strawberries for the commercial market in the United States, the volatile compounds, methyl anthranilate and gamma-decalactone prominent in aromatic wild strawberries, are especially desired for their "sweet and fruity" aroma characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Negri_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Negri-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemicals present in the fragrance of strawberries include:
</p>

</div>
<p>As strawberry flavor and <a href="/wiki/Fragrance" class="mw-redirect" title="Fragrance">fragrance</a> are characteristics that may appeal to consumers,<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Negri_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Negri-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> they are used widely in a variety of manufacturing, including foods, beverages, <a href="/wiki/Confection" class="mw-redirect" title="Confection">confections</a>, perfumes and cosmetics.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>Modern strawberries are <a href="/wiki/Octaploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Octaploid">octoploid</a> (8 sets of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> sequence of the garden strawberry was published in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">Allergy</span></h2>
<p>Some people experience an <a href="/wiki/Anaphylactoid_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphylactoid reaction">anaphylactoid reaction</a> to eating strawberries.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The most common form of this reaction is <a href="/wiki/Oral_allergy_syndrome" title="Oral allergy syndrome">oral allergy syndrome</a>, but symptoms may also mimic <a href="/wiki/Hay_fever" class="mw-redirect" title="Hay fever">hay fever</a> or include <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hives" title="Hives">hives</a>, and, in severe cases, may cause breathing problems.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proteomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteomic">Proteomic</a> studies indicate that the allergen may be tied to a protein for the red anthocyanin biosynthesis expressed in strawberry ripening, named Fra a1 (Fragaria allergen1).<sup id="cite_ref-Munoz_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munoz-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">Homologous</a> proteins are found in <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> and apple, suggesting that people may develop <a href="/wiki/Cross-reactivity" title="Cross-reactivity">cross-reactivity</a> to all three species.
</p><p>White-fruited strawberry <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a>, lacking Fra a1, may be an option for strawberry allergy sufferers. Since they lack a protein necessary for normal ripening by anthocyanin synthesis of red pigments, they do not turn the mature berries of other cultivars red.<sup id="cite_ref-Munoz_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munoz-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> They ripen but remain white, pale yellow or "golden", appearing like immature berries; this also has the advantage of making them less attractive to birds. A virtually allergen-free cultivar named 'Sofar' is available.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h2>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif/lossy-page1-220px-%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif/lossy-page1-330px-%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif/lossy-page1-440px-%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BF%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%86%D1%8B.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="943" /></a><figcaption>Pollen grains of strawberry</figcaption></figure>
<p>Strawberries are often grouped according to their flowering habit.<sup id="cite_ref-missouri1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-missouri1-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sagers_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sagers-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally, this has consisted of a division between "June-bearing" strawberries, which bear their fruit in the early summer and "ever-bearing" strawberries, which often bear several crops of fruit throughout the season.<sup id="cite_ref-sagers_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sagers-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> One plant throughout a season may produce 50 to 60 times or roughly once every three days.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research published in 2001 showed that strawberries actually occur in three basic flowering habits: short-day, long-day, and day-neutral. These refer to the day-length sensitivity of the plant and the type of <a href="/wiki/Photoperiod" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoperiod">photoperiod</a> that induces flower formation. Day-neutral cultivars produce flowers regardless of the photoperiod.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
Strawberry cultivars vary widely in size, color, flavor, shape, degree of fertility, season of ripening, liability to disease and constitution of plant.<sup id="cite_ref-missouri1_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-missouri1-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On average, a strawberry has about 200 seeds on its external membrane.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Some vary in foliage, and some vary materially in the relative development of their sexual organs. In most cases, the flowers appear <a href="/wiki/Hermaphroditic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermaphroditic">hermaphroditic</a> in structure, but function as either male or female.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg/220px-Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg/330px-Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg/440px-Strawberries_and_cream_Wimbledon_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1346" /></a><figcaption>Strawberries and cream</figcaption></figure>
<p>In addition to being consumed fresh, strawberries can be frozen or made into jam or <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">preserves</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Giampieri2013_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giampieri2013-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> as well as dried and used in prepared foods, such as cereal bars.<sup id="cite_ref-StrawberryOatmealBarsRecipeFoodNetwork_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StrawberryOatmealBarsRecipeFoodNetwork-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Strawberries and strawberry flavorings are a popular addition to dairy products, such as <a href="/wiki/Flavored_milk" title="Flavored milk">strawberry milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_ice_cream" title="Strawberry ice cream">strawberry ice cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milkshakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Milkshakes">strawberry milkshakes</a>/<a href="/wiki/Smoothies" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoothies">smoothies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">strawberry yogurts</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, "strawberries and cream" is a popular dessert consumed at the <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a> tennis tournament.<sup id="cite_ref-Wimbledon_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wimbledon-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Strawberries and cream is also a staple snack in Mexico, usually available at ice cream parlors. In Sweden, strawberries are a traditional dessert served on <a href="/wiki/Midsummer" title="Midsummer">Midsummer</a>'s Eve. Depending on area, <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_pie" title="Strawberry pie">strawberry pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhubarb_pie" title="Rhubarb pie">strawberry rhubarb pie</a>, or strawberry <a href="/wiki/Shortcake" title="Shortcake">shortcake</a> are also common. In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, strawberries may be sprinkled with sugar and then dipped in <a href="/wiki/Metaxa" title="Metaxa">Metaxa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a>, and served as a dessert. In Italy, strawberries are used for various desserts and as a common flavoring for <a href="/wiki/Gelato" title="Gelato">gelato</a> (gelato alla fragola).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Fresh strawberries are used as <a href="/wiki/Cocktail_garnish" title="Cocktail garnish">cocktail garnishes</a> to add flavor and texture to a drink.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">Polyphenol</a> extracted from strawberries have been used in <a href="/wiki/Kanazawa" title="Kanazawa">Kanazawa</a>, Japan, to create <a href="/wiki/Melting" title="Melting">melting</a>-resistant <a href="/wiki/Ice_pop" title="Ice pop">popsicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Huang_2017_v148_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang_2017_v148-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h3>

scope="row" class="infobox-label">World total</th><td class="infobox-data">9.18</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below">Source: <a href="/wiki/FAOSTAT" class="mw-redirect" title="FAOSTAT">FAOSTAT</a><sup id="cite_ref-faostat_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 2021, world production of strawberries was 9.2&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China with 37% of the total and the United States and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> as other significant producers (table).
</p><p>Due to the relatively fragile nature of the strawberry, approximately 35 percent of the $2.2 billion United States crop was spoiled in 2020. This led to an <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> company planning to launch gene-edited strawberries in the near future in an effort to make them more durable. In the U.S. it costs growers around $35,000 per acre to plant and $35,000 per acre to harvest strawberries now, and more durable berries might reduce the rate of spoilage.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For purposes of commercial production, plants are propagated from <a href="/wiki/Stolon" title="Stolon">runners</a> and, in general, distributed as either bare root plants or plugs. Cultivation follows one of two general models—annual <a href="/wiki/Plasticulture" title="Plasticulture">plasticulture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-osu-2126_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-2126-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> or a perennial system of matted rows or mounds.<sup id="cite_ref-nevfc_production_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevfc_production-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Greenhouses produce a small amount of strawberries during the off season.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The bulk of modern commercial production uses the plasticulture system. In this method, raised beds are formed each year, <a href="/wiki/Fumigation" title="Fumigation">fumigated</a>, and covered with plastic to prevent weed growth and erosion. Plants, usually obtained from northern nurseries, are planted through holes punched in this covering, and irrigation tubing is run underneath. Runners are removed from the plants as they appear, to encourage the plants to put most of their energy into fruit development. After harvesting, the plastic is removed and the plants are plowed into the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-osu-2126_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-2126-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-noble_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noble-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Strawberry plants produce more and better fruit when they are young. After a year or two, they decline. Replacing them annually improves yields and enables denser planting.<sup id="cite_ref-osu-2126_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-2126-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-noble_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noble-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> However, this necessitates a longer growing season, for the plants to establish themselves.  It also costs more to annually purchase plants, form new mounds, and cover them with (new) plastic.<sup id="cite_ref-noble_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noble-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The other major method retains plants for multiple years. This is most common in colder climates. The plants are grown in rows or on mounds.<sup id="cite_ref-osu-2126_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-2126-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nevfc_production_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevfc_production-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> This method requires lower investment and lower maintenance, overall.<sup id="cite_ref-nevfc_production_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevfc_production-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Yields are typically lower than in plasticulture.<sup id="cite_ref-nevfc_production_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevfc_production-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another method uses a <a href="/wiki/Uses_of_compost#Erosion_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Uses of compost">compost sock</a>. Plants grown in compost socks have been shown to produce significantly more <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malic_acid" title="Malic acid">malic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a> than fruit produced in the black <a href="/wiki/Plastic_mulch" title="Plastic mulch">plastic mulch</a> or matted row systems.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Similar results in an earlier study conducted by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> confirms how compost plays a role in the bioactive qualities of two strawberry cultivars.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Strawberries may also be propagated by seed, though this is primarily a hobby activity, and is not widely practiced commercially. A few seed-propagated cultivars have been developed for home use, and research into growing from seed commercially is ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Seeds (<a href="/wiki/Achene" title="Achene">achenes</a>) are acquired either via commercial seed suppliers, or by collecting and saving them from the fruit.
</p><p>Strawberries can also be grown indoors in strawberry pots.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Strawberries will not grow indoors in winter unless aided by a combination of blue and red <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, winter is the natural growing season and harvesting begins in mid-November.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Kashubian strawberry (<i>Truskawka kaszubska</i> or <i>Kaszëbskô malëna</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> is the first Polish fruit to be given commercial protection under EU law. It is produced in <a href="/wiki/Kartuzy_County" title="Kartuzy County">Kartuzy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bcierzyna_County" title="Kościerzyna County">Kościerzyna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byt%C3%B3w_County" title="Bytów County">Bytów</a> counties and in the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Przywidz,_Pomeranian_Voivodeship" title="Przywidz, Pomeranian Voivodeship">Przywidz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wejherowo" title="Wejherowo">Wejherowo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luzino" title="Luzino">Luzino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Szemud" title="Szemud">Szemud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linia,_Pomeranian_Voivodeship" title="Linia, Pomeranian Voivodeship">Linia</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C5%81%C4%99czyce" class="mw-redirect" title="Łęczyce">Łęczyce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cewice" title="Cewice">Cewice</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kashubia" title="Kashubia">Kashubia</a>. Only the following varieties may be sold as <i>kaszëbskô malëna</i>: Senga Sengana, Elsanta, Honeoye that have been graded as Extra or Class I.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px">

		</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuring_and_harvesting">Manuring and harvesting</span></h3>
<p>Most strawberry plants are now fed with <a href="/wiki/Artificial_fertilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial fertilizer">artificial fertilizers</a>, both before and after harvesting, and often before planting in <a href="/wiki/Plasticulture" title="Plasticulture">plasticulture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To maintain top quality, berries are harvested at least every other day. The berries are picked with the caps still attached and with at least half an inch of stem left. Strawberries need to remain on the plant to fully ripen because they do not continue to ripen after being picked. Rotted and overripe berries are removed to minimize insect and disease problems. The berries do not get washed until just before consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec,_Canada.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A man carries a flat of strawberries in a field" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg/220px-Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg/330px-Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg/440px-Picker_on_a_strawberry_field_in_Quebec%2C_Canada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5189" data-file-height="3538" /></a><figcaption>Strawberries are usually picked and placed in shallow boxes in the field.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Soil test information and plant analysis results are used to determine fertility practices. Nitrogen fertilizer is needed at the beginning of every planting year. There are normally adequate levels of phosphorus and potash when fields have been fertilized for top yields. To provide more organic matter, a cover crop of wheat or rye is planted in the winter before planting the strawberries. Strawberries prefer a pH from 5.5 to 6.5, so lime is usually not applied.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The harvesting and cleaning process has not changed substantially over time. The delicate strawberries are still harvested by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-6237_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-6237-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Grading and packing often occurs in the field, rather than in a processing facility.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-6237_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-6237-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In large operations, strawberries are cleaned by means of water streams and shaking conveyor belts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_cultivation">Domestic cultivation</span></h3>
<p>Strawberries are popular in home gardens, and numerous <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> have been selected for consumption and for exhibition purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The following cultivars have gained the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Award_of_Garden_Merit" title="Award of Garden Merit">Award of Garden Merit</a>:
</p>
<table>

<tbody><tr valign="top">
<td>
<ul><li>'Cambridge Favourite'<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>'Hapil'<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>'Honeoye' (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ʌ/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">ʌ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ɔɪ/: &#39;oi&#39; in &#39;choice&#39;">ɔɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">HUN</span>-ee-oy</i></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG/220px-Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG/330px-Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG/440px-Strawberries_at_Ljubljana_Central_Market.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4592" data-file-height="2576" /></a><figcaption>Fresh strawberries being sold in plastic containers</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the United States in 2017, the collective commercial production of strawberries, <a href="/wiki/Blueberry" title="Blueberry">blueberries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raspberry" title="Raspberry">raspberries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blackberry" title="Blackberry">blackberries</a> was a $6&#160;billion industry dominated by the California growing and marketing company <a href="/wiki/Driscoll%27s" title="Driscoll&#39;s">Driscoll's</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, strawberries alone were a $3.5&#160;billion market of which 82% was for fresh fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-amrc_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amrc-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To increase consumer demand in the 21st century, commercial producers of strawberries cultivated them mainly for favorable aroma characteristics similar to those of wild strawberries,<sup id="cite_ref-Negri_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Negri-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> in addition to having large size, heart-shape, glossy red exterior, firmness, and slow ripening for long <a href="/wiki/Shelf-life" class="mw-redirect" title="Shelf-life">shelf-life</a> favorable to ship by <a href="/wiki/Ground_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground transportation">ground transportation</a> from farms to stores nationwide for consumption within two weeks of harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In US and Canadian <a href="/wiki/Grocery_store" title="Grocery store">grocery stores</a>, fresh strawberries are typically sold in plastic <a href="/wiki/Clamshell_(container)" title="Clamshell (container)">clamshells</a>, and are among the top fresh produce items in grocery revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> One marketing analysis identified strawberries and other berries as a source of "happiness" for consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodyear_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span></h3>
<p>Around 200 species of <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)#Classification_by_harmed_organisms_or_ecosystems" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a> are known to attack strawberries both directly and indirectly.<sup id="cite_ref-strawmaster_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strawmaster-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> These pests include <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drosophilidae" title="Drosophilidae">fruit flies</a>, chafers, strawberry root weevils, strawberry thrips, strawberry sap beetles, strawberry crown moth, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-strawmaster_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strawmaster-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The caterpillars of a number of species of <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a> feed on strawberry plants. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Ghost_moth" title="Ghost moth">ghost moth</a> is known to be a pest of the strawberry plant.
</p><p>The strawberry aphid, <i><a href="/wiki/Chaetosiphon_fragaefolii" title="Chaetosiphon fragaefolii">Chaetosiphon fragaefolii</a></i>, is a bug species found in the United States (Arizona), Argentina and Chile. It is a vector of the <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_mild_yellow-edge_virus" title="Strawberry mild yellow-edge virus">strawberry mild yellow-edge virus</a>.
</p><p>The amounts of <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> required for <a href="/wiki/Agroindustry" class="mw-redirect" title="Agroindustry">industrial production</a> of strawberries (300 pounds (140&#160;kg) in California per acre) have led to the strawberry leading the list of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Working_Group" title="Environmental Working Group">EWG</a>'s "Dirty Dozen" of pesticide-contaminated produce.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_strawberry_diseases" title="List of strawberry diseases">List of strawberry diseases</a></div>
<p>Strawberry plants can fall victim to a number of diseases, especially when subjected to stress.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-1148_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-1148-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The leaves may be infected by <a href="/wiki/Powdery_mildew" title="Powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leaf_spot" title="Leaf spot">leaf spot</a> (caused by the fungus <i>Sphaerella fragariae</i>), <a href="/wiki/Leaf_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaf blight">leaf blight</a> (caused by the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Phomopsis_obscurans" title="Phomopsis obscurans">Phomopsis obscurans</a></i>), and by a variety of <a href="/wiki/Slime_mold" title="Slime mold">slime molds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-1148_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-1148-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The crown and roots may fall victim to red stele, <a href="/wiki/Verticillium" title="Verticillium">verticillium</a> wilt, <a href="/wiki/Thielaviopsis" title="Thielaviopsis">black root rot</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-1148_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-1148-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The fruits are subject to damage from gray mold (<i><a href="/wiki/Botrytis_cinerea" title="Botrytis cinerea">Botrytis cinerea</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Rhizopus" title="Rhizopus">rhizopus</a> rot, and leather rot.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-1148_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-1148-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> To prevent root-rotting, strawberries should be planted every four to five years in a new bed, at a different site.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="AtNPR1"></span><span class="vanchor-text"><i>NPR1</i> gene from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></span></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/AtNPR1" class="mw-redirect" title="AtNPR1">AtNPR1</a></i>, confers <i>A. thaliana</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s <a href="/wiki/Broad-spectrum_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Broad-spectrum resistance">broad-spectrum resistance</a> when <a href="/wiki/Transexpression" class="mw-redirect" title="Transexpression">transexpressed</a> in <i>F. ananassa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR-trans_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-trans-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> This resistance includes resistance to anthracnose, powdery mildew, and angular leaf spot.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR-trans_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-trans-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 1997 study assessed many <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711"><span class="vanchor"><span id="Wound_volatile"></span><span class="vanchor-text"><a href="/wiki/Wound_volatiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Wound volatiles">wound volatiles</a></span></span> and found all effective against gray mold (<i>B. cinerea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Wound-Volatile_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wound-Volatile-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Tribute_(variety)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribute (variety)">Tribute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chandler_(variety)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chandler (variety)">Chandler</a> were tested and benefited from the treatments, although there are large differences between the substance x variety effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Wound-Volatile_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wound-Volatile-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Strawberry <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolizes</a> these volatiles, and does so more rapidly than either <a href="/wiki/Blackberry" title="Blackberry">blackberry</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wound-Volatile_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wound-Volatile-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The plants can also develop disease from temperature extremes during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-umn-1148_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umn-1148-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Watering strawberry roots, and not the leaves, is preferred as moisture on leaves encourages fungal growth.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Strawberries may also often appear conjoined together or deformed due to poor pollination.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
